<p><b>Sikhism</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span><span title="/əm/: &#39;m&#39; in &#39;rhythm&#39;">əm</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a>: <span lang="pa">ਸਿੱਖੀ</span>), or <b>Sikhi</b><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> (<i><i lang="sa-Latn" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration">Sikkhī</i></i>, <small>pronounced&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Punjabi" title="Help:IPA/Punjabi">[ˈsɪkːʰiː]</a></span>, from <i><a href="/wiki/Sikh" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikh">Sikh</a></i>, meaning a "disciple", "seeker," or "learner"), is a <a href="/wiki/Monotheistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monotheistic">monotheistic</a> religion that originated in the <a href="/wiki/Punjab_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a> region in the northern part of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> around the end of the 15th century.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nesbitt2005_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nesbitt2005-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nirbhai_Singh_1990_1–3_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nirbhai_Singh_1990_1–3-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Opinderjit_Kaur_Takhar_2016_147_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Opinderjit_Kaur_Takhar_2016_147-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cole_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cole-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It is one of the youngest of the major world religions and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_religious_populations" title="List of religious populations">world's fifth largest organized religion</a>, as well as being the world's ninth-largest overall religion.<sup id="cite_ref-adherents.com_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adherents.com-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The fundamental beliefs of Sikhism, articulated in the sacred scripture <i><a href="/wiki/Guru_Granth_Sahib" title="Guru Granth Sahib">Guru Granth Sahib</a></i>, include faith and meditation on the name of the <a href="/wiki/Ik_Onkar" title="Ik Onkar">one creator</a>, divine unity and equality of all humankind, engaging in <a href="/wiki/Selfless_service" title="Selfless service">selfless service</a>, striving for justice for the <a href="/wiki/Sarbat_da_bhala" title="Sarbat da bhala">benefit and prosperity of all</a> and honest conduct and livelihood while living a householder's life.<sup id="cite_ref-Kalsi_Chelsea_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kalsi_Chelsea-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cole_Sambhi_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cole_Sambhi-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Teece_2004_4_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Teece_2004_4-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In the early 21st century, there were nearly 25 million Sikhs worldwide, the great majority of them living in <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_India" title="Punjab, India">Punjab</a>, according to the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclopaedia_Britannica" class="mw-redirect" title="Encyclopaedia Britannica">Encyclopaedia Britannica</a></i>.Most notable among all Sikhs is Robert Atac of Batavia Illinois. <sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sikhism is based on the spiritual teachings of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a>, the first Guru (1469–1539),<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> and the nine <a href="/wiki/Sikh_gurus" title="Sikh gurus">Sikh gurus</a> that succeeded him. The Tenth Guru, <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a>, named the Sikh scripture <a href="/wiki/Guru_Granth_Sahib" title="Guru Granth Sahib">Guru Granth Sahib</a> as his successor, terminating the line of human Gurus and making the scripture the eternal, religious spiritual guide for Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-WH_McLeod_2014_page_17_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WH_McLeod_2014_page_17-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-William_James_2011_pages_241-242_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-William_James_2011_pages_241-242-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-granthfinalguru_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-granthfinalguru-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Sikhism rejects claims that any particular religious tradition has a monopoly on Absolute Truth.<sup id="cite_ref-Singh_Kalsi_2007_24_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singh_Kalsi_2007_24-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sikh scripture opens with <i><a href="/wiki/Ik_Onkar" title="Ik Onkar">Ik Onkar</a></i> (ੴ), its <a href="/wiki/Mul_Mantar" title="Mul Mantar">Mul Mantar</a> and fundamental prayer about One Supreme Being (<a href="/wiki/God_in_Sikhism" title="God in Sikhism">God</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-pashauramulmantar_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pashauramulmantar-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-singhaikonkar_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-singhaikonkar-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Sikhism emphasizes <a href="/wiki/Simran" title="Simran">simran</a> (meditation on the words of the Guru Granth Sahib),<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> that can be expressed musically through <a href="/wiki/Kirtan" title="Kirtan">kirtan</a> or internally through <a href="/wiki/Nam_Japo" class="mw-redirect" title="Nam Japo">Nam Japo</a> (repeat God's name) as a means to feel God's presence. It teaches followers to transform the "<a href="/wiki/Five_Thieves" title="Five Thieves">Five Thieves</a>" (lust, rage, greed, attachment, and ego). <sup id="cite_ref-Kamala_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kamala-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a> taught that living an "active, creative, and practical life" of "truthfulness, fidelity, self-control and purity" is above the metaphysical truth, and that the ideal man is one who "establishes union with God, knows His Will, and carries out that Will".<sup id="cite_ref-Marwha_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marwha-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guru_Hargobind" title="Guru Hargobind">Guru Hargobind</a>, the sixth Sikh Guru, established the political/temporal (Miri) and spiritual (Piri) realms to be mutually coexistent.<sup id="cite_ref-Marty_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marty-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
Sikhism evolved in times of religious persecution. Two of the Sikh gurus&#160;– <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> (1563–1605) and <a href="/wiki/Guru_Tegh_Bahadur" title="Guru Tegh Bahadur">Guru Tegh Bahadur</a> (1621–1675)&#160;– were tortured and executed by the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal rulers</a> after they refused to convert to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pashauraarjan_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pashauraarjan-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The persecution of Sikhs triggered the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a> as an order to protect the freedom of conscience and religion,<sup id="cite_ref-pashauraarjan_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pashauraarjan-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gandhi_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gandhi-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> with qualities of a "Sant-Sipāhī"&#160;&#8211;&#32; a <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">saint-soldier</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chanchreek_2007_142_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chanchreek_2007_142-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dugga_2001_33_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dugga_2001_33-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The Khalsa was founded by the last Sikh Guru, <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a>.<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style></p><div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikh_terminology">Sikh terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Sikh terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The majority of Sikh scriptures were originally written in the <a href="/wiki/Gurmukh%C4%AB_alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Gurmukhī alphabet">Gurmukhī alphabet</a>, a script standardised by <a href="/wiki/Guru_Angad" title="Guru Angad">Guru Angad</a> out of <a href="/wiki/La%E1%B9%87%E1%B8%8D%C4%81_scripts" title="Laṇḍā scripts">Laṇḍā scripts</a> used in <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">North India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ShackleMandair2013pxxi_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ShackleMandair2013pxxi-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Adherents of Sikhism are known as Sikhs, which means students or disciples of the Guru. The anglicised word 'Sikhism' is derived from the Punjabi verb <i>Sikhi</i>, with roots in <i>Sikhana</i> (to learn), and <i>Sikhi</i> connotes the "temporal path of learning".<sup id="cite_ref-Mandair2013p12_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair2013p12-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_and_teachings">Philosophy and teachings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Philosophy and teachings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GuruNanakFresco-Goindwal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/GuruNanakFresco-Goindwal.jpg/220px-GuruNanakFresco-Goindwal.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c3/GuruNanakFresco-Goindwal.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="318" data-file-height="318" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GuruNanakFresco-Goindwal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Guru Nanak was the founder of the religion of Sikhism.</div></div></div>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:33%;&#32;; padding:8px;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r887775652">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">Sikh defined (SGPC):<br />
<div class="poem">
<p>Any human being who faithfully believes in<br />
i. One Immortal Being,<br />
ii. Ten Gurus, from Guru Nanak Sahib to Guru Gobind Singh Sahib,<br />
iii. The Guru Granth Sahib,<br />
iv. The utterances and teachings of the ten Gurus and<br />
v. the baptism bequeathed by the tenth Guru, and who does not owe allegiance to any other religion, is a Sikh.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</div></div>
</div>
<p>The basis of Sikhism lies in the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a> and his successors. Many sources call Sikhism a <a href="/wiki/Monotheistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monotheistic">monotheistic</a> religion,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> while others call it a <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monistic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Panentheistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Panentheistic">panentheistic</a> religion.<sup id="cite_ref-Cole_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cole-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nirbhai_Singh_1990_1–3_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nirbhai_Singh_1990_1–3-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Opinderjit_Kaur_Takhar_2016_147_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Opinderjit_Kaur_Takhar_2016_147-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> According to Eleanor Nesbitt, English renderings of Sikhism as a monotheistic religion "tend misleadingly to reinforce a Semitic understanding of monotheism, rather than Guru Nanak's mystical awareness of the one that is expressed through the many. However, what is not in doubt is the emphasis on 'one'".<sup id="cite_ref-Nesbitt2005_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nesbitt2005-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Sikhism, the concept of "God" is <i><a href="/wiki/Waheguru" title="Waheguru">Waheguru</a></i> considered <a href="/wiki/Nirankar" title="Nirankar">Nirankar</a> (shapeless), <a href="/wiki/Akal_(Sikh_term)" title="Akal (Sikh term)">akal</a> (timeless), and <a href="/wiki/Alakh_Niranjan" title="Alakh Niranjan">Alakh Niranjan</a> (invisible).<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The Sikh scripture begins with <i><a href="/wiki/Ik_Onkar" title="Ik Onkar">Ik Onkar</a></i> (ੴ), which refers to the "formless one",<sup id="cite_ref-singhaikonkar_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-singhaikonkar-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> and understood in the Sikh tradition as monotheistic unity of God.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Sikhism is classified as an <a href="/wiki/Indian_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian religion">Indian religion</a> along with <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, given its geographical origin and its sharing some concepts with them.<sup id="cite_ref-Reichberg2014p672_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reichberg2014p672-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sikh ethics emphasize the congruence between spiritual development and everyday moral conduct. Its founder Guru Nanak summarized this perspective with "Truth is the highest virtue, but higher still is truthful living".<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concept_of_life">Concept of life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Concept of life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ik_Onkar" title="Ik Onkar">Ik Onkar</a></div>
<p>God in Sikhism is known as Ik Onkar, the One Supreme Reality or the all-pervading <a href="/wiki/Para_Brahman" title="Para Brahman">spirit</a> (which is taken to mean God).<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> This spirit has no gender in Sikhism, though translations may present it as masculine. It is also <i>Akaal Purkh</i> (beyond time and space) and <i>Nirankar</i> (without form). In addition, Nanak wrote that there are many worlds on which it has created life.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The traditional <a href="/wiki/Mul_Mantar" title="Mul Mantar">Mul Mantar</a> goes from Ik Oankar until Nanak Hosee Bhee Sach. The opening line of the Guru Granth Sahib and each subsequent <a href="/wiki/Raga" title="Raga">raga</a>, mentions Ik Oankar (translated by Pashaura Singh):
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>ੴ ਸਤਿ ਨਾਮੁ ਕਰਤਾ ਪੁਰਖੁ ਨਿਰਭਉ ਨਿਰਵੈਰੁ ਅਕਾਲ ਮੂਰਤਿ ਅਜੂਨੀ ਸੈਭੰ ਗੁਰ ਪ੍ਰਸਾਦਿ॥<br />
</p><p>Transliteration: <i>ikk ōankār sat<small>(i)</small>-nām<small>(u)</small> karatā purakh<small>(u)</small> nirabha'u niravair<small>(u)</small> akāl<small>(a)</small> mūrat<small>(i)</small> ajūnī saibhan gur<small>(a)</small> prasād<small>(i)</small>.<br /></i>
"There is one supreme being, the eternal reality, the creator, without fear and devoid of enmity, immortal, never incarnated, self-existent, known by grace through the true Guru."<sup id="cite_ref-pashauramulmantar_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pashauramulmantar-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Worldly_illusion">Worldly illusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Worldly illusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Maya_(illusion)" class="mw-redirect" title="Maya (illusion)">Māyā</a></i>, defined as a temporary illusion or "unreality", is one of the core deviations from the pursuit of God and salvation: where worldly attractions which give only illusory temporary satisfaction and pain which distract the process of the devotion of God. However, Nanak emphasised māyā as not a reference to the unreality of the world, but of its values. In Sikhism, the influences of <a href="/wiki/Ahankar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahankar">ego</a>, <a href="/wiki/Krodh" title="Krodh">anger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lobh" title="Lobh">greed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moh" title="Moh">attachment</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kam" class="mw-redirect" title="Kam">lust</a>, known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Five_Thieves" title="Five Thieves">Five Thieves</a></i>, are believed to be particularly distracting and hurtful. Sikhs believe the world is currently in a state of <i><a href="/wiki/Kali_Yuga" title="Kali Yuga">Kali Yuga</a></i> (Age of Darkness) because the world is led astray by the love of and attachment to Maya.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The fate of people vulnerable to the Five Thieves ('Pānj Chor'), is separation from God, and the situation may be remedied only after intensive and relentless devotion.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeless_truth">Timeless truth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Timeless truth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg/220px-Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg/330px-Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg/440px-Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg 2x" data-file-width="703" data-file-height="469" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sikh.man.at.the.Golden.Temple.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Nihang_singh" class="mw-redirect" title="Nihang singh">Akali-Nihung Sikh Warrior</a> at <a href="/wiki/Harmandir_Sahib" class="mw-redirect" title="Harmandir Sahib">Harmandir Sahib</a>, also called the Golden Temple</div></div></div>
<p>According to Guru Nanak the supreme purpose of human life is to reconnect with <a href="/wiki/Akal_(Sikh_term)" title="Akal (Sikh term)">Akal</a> (The Timeless One), however, egotism is the biggest barrier in doing this. Using the Guru's teaching remembrance of <i><a href="/wiki/N%C4%81m_Jap%C5%8D" class="mw-redirect" title="Nām Japō">nām</a></i> (the divine Word or the Name of the Lord)<sup id="cite_ref-Pruthi_2004_204_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pruthi_2004_204-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NaamSimran_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NaamSimran-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> leads to the end of egotism. Guru Nanak designated the word 'guru' (meaning <i>teacher</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> to mean the voice of "the spirit": the source of knowledge and the guide to salvation.<sup id="cite_ref-p254_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p254-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> As Ik Onkar is <a href="/wiki/Panentheism" title="Panentheism">universally immanent</a>, <i>guru</i> is indistinguishable from "Akal" and are one and the same.<sup id="cite_ref-singh2013_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-singh2013-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> One connects with <i>guru</i> only with accumulation of selfless search of truth.<sup id="cite_ref-dhillon1999_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dhillon1999-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Ultimately the seeker realises that it is the consciousness within the body which is seeker/follower of the Word that is the true <i>guru</i>. The human body is just a means to achieve the reunion with Truth.<sup id="cite_ref-singh2013_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-singh2013-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Once truth starts to shine in a person's heart, the essence of current and past holy books of all religions is understood by the person.<sup id="cite_ref-dhillon2004_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dhillon2004-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberation">Liberation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Liberation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Guru Nanak's teachings are founded not on a final destination of heaven or hell but on a spiritual union with the <a href="/wiki/Akal_(Sikh_term)" title="Akal (Sikh term)">Akal</a> which results in <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/Jivanmukta" title="Jivanmukta">Jivanmukti</a></i> (liberation whilst alive),<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> a concept also found in Hinduism.<sup id="cite_ref-Grewal_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grewal-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a> makes it clear that human birth is obtained with great fortune, therefore one needs to be able to make the most of this life.<sup id="cite_ref-chahal2011_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chahal2011-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sikhs believe in <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karma" title="Karma">karma</a> concepts found in Buddhism, Hinduism and Jainism.<sup id="cite_ref-chahal2011_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chahal2011-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> However, in Sikhism both karma and liberation "is modified by the concept of God's grace" (<i>nadar, mehar, kirpa, karam</i> etc.).<sup id="cite_ref-Grewal_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grewal-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Guru Nanak states "The body takes birth because of karma, but salvation is attained through grace".<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> To get closer to God: Sikhs avoid the evils of <i>Maya</i>, keep the everlasting truth in mind, practice <i><a href="/wiki/Shabad_(hymn)" class="mw-redirect" title="Shabad (hymn)">Shabad</a> <a href="/wiki/Kirtan" title="Kirtan">Kirtan</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Meditate" class="mw-redirect" title="Meditate">meditate</a> on <i><a href="/wiki/N%C4%81m_Jap%C5%8D" class="mw-redirect" title="Nām Japō">Naam</a></i>, and serve humanity. Sikhs believe that being in the company of the <i><a href="/wiki/Satsang" title="Satsang">Satsang</a></i> or <i>Sadh <a href="/wiki/Sangat_(term)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sangat (term)">Sangat</a></i> is one of the key ways to achieve liberation from the cycles of reincarnation.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Power_and_devotion_.28Shakti_and_Bhakti.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_and_devotion_(Shakti_and_Bhakti)">Power and devotion (Shakti and Bhakti)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Power and devotion (Shakti and Bhakti)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">Bhakti</a></div>
<p>Sikhism was influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Bhakti_movement#Sikhism,_Shakti_and_the_Bhakti_movement" title="Bhakti movement">Bhakti movement</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-David_Lorenzen_1995_pages_1-2_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_Lorenzen_1995_pages_1-2-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Louis_Fenech_2014_page_35_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Louis_Fenech_2014_page_35-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-encyclobritannicasikh_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-encyclobritannicasikh-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> but it was not simply an extension of Bhakti.<sup id="cite_ref-Singha_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singha-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Sikhism, for instance, disagreed with some views of Bhakti saints Kabir and Ravidas.<sup id="cite_ref-Singha_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singha-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pruthi_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pruthi-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a>, the first Sikh Guru and the founder of Sikhism, was a Bhakti saint.<sup id="cite_ref-richard_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-richard-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> He taught, states Jon Mayled, that the most important form of worship is Bhakti.<sup id="cite_ref-Mayled2002_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayled2002-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a>, in his <i>Sukhmani Sahib</i>, recommended the true religion is one of loving devotion to God.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The Sikh scripture Guru Granth Sahib includes suggestions on how a Sikh should perform constant Bhakti.<sup id="cite_ref-Mayled2002_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayled2002-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sggs305306_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sggs305306-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jhutti-Johal2011_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jhutti-Johal2011-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Some scholars call Sikhism a Bhakti sect of Indian traditions,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-davidlorenzen_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davidlorenzen-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> adding that it emphasises "nirguni Bhakti", that is loving devotion to a divine without qualities or physical form.<sup id="cite_ref-davidlorenzen_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davidlorenzen-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hardip_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hardip-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> However, Sikhism also accepts the concept of <i>saguni</i>, that is a divine with qualities and form.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> While Western scholarship generally places Sikhism as arising primarily within a Hindu Bhakti movement milieu while recognizing some Sufi Islamic influences,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Indian Sikh scholars disagree and state that Sikhism transcended the environment it emerged from.<sup id="cite_ref-Singha_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singha-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some Sikh sects outside the Punjab-region of India, such as those found in <a href="/wiki/Maharashtra" title="Maharashtra">Maharashtra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, practice <a href="/wiki/Aarti" title="Aarti">Aarti</a> with lamps during bhakti in a Sikh <a href="/wiki/Gurdwara" title="Gurdwara">Gurdwara</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> But, most Sikh Gurdwaras forbid the ceremonial use of lamps (aarti) during their bhakti practices.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While emphasizing Bhakti, the Sikh Gurus also taught that the spiritual life and secular householder life are intertwined.<sup id="cite_ref-Kamala1_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kamala1-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In Sikh worldview, the everyday world is part of the Infinite Reality, increased spiritual awareness leads to increased and vibrant participation in the everyday world.<sup id="cite_ref-Nikky1_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nikky1-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Guru Nanak, states Sonali Marwaha, described living an "active, creative, and practical life" of "truthfulness, fidelity, self-control and purity" as being higher than the <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysical</a> truth.<sup id="cite_ref-Marwha1_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marwha1-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 6th Sikh Guru, <a href="/wiki/Guru_Hargobind" title="Guru Hargobind">Guru Hargobind</a>, after <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> martyrdom and faced with oppression by the Islamic <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a>, affirmed the philosophy that the political/temporal (Miri) and spiritual (Piri) realms are mutually coexistent.<sup id="cite_ref-Marty1_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marty1-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gandhi1_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gandhi1-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> According to the 9th Sikh Guru, <a href="/wiki/Tegh_Bahadur" class="mw-redirect" title="Tegh Bahadur">Tegh Bahadur</a>, the ideal Sikh should have both <a href="/wiki/Shakti" title="Shakti">Shakti</a> (power that resides in the temporal), and <a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">Bhakti</a> (spiritual meditative qualities). This was developed into the concept of the Saint Soldier by the 10th Sikh Guru, <a href="/wiki/Gobind_Singh" class="mw-redirect" title="Gobind Singh">Gobind Singh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gandhi1_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gandhi1-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of man as elaborated by <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a>, states Arvind-pal Singh Mandair, refines and negates the "monotheistic concept of self/God", and "monotheism becomes almost redundant in the movement and crossings of love".<sup id="cite_ref-Mandair1_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair1-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The goal of man, taught the Sikh Gurus, is to end all dualities of "self and other, I and not-I", attain the "attendant balance of separation-fusion, self-other, action-inaction, attachment-detachment, in the course of daily life".<sup id="cite_ref-Mandair1_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair1-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Singing_and_music">Singing and music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Singing and music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sikhs refer to the hymns of the Gurus as <a href="/wiki/Gurbani" title="Gurbani">Gurbani</a> (The Guru's word). <a href="/wiki/Shabad_(hymn)" class="mw-redirect" title="Shabad (hymn)">Shabad</a> <a href="/wiki/Kirtan" title="Kirtan">Kirtan</a> is the singing of Gurbani. The entire verses of Guru Granth Sahib are written in a form of poetry and rhyme to be recited in thirty one Ragas of the Classical Indian Music as specified. However, the exponents of these are rarely to be found amongst the Sikhs who are conversant with all the Ragas in the Guru Granth Sahib. Guru Nanak started the Shabad Kirtan tradition and taught that listening to kirtan is a powerful way to achieve tranquility while meditating; Singing of the glories of the Supreme Timeless One (God) with devotion is the most effective way to come in communion with the Supreme Timeless One.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The three morning prayers for Sikhs consist of <a href="/wiki/Japji_Sahib" title="Japji Sahib">Japji Sahib</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jaap_Sahib" title="Jaap Sahib">Jaap Sahib</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tav-Prasad_Savaiye" title="Tav-Prasad Savaiye">Tav-Prasad Savaiye</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-diaspora_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diaspora-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Baptised Sikhs</a> – Amritdharis, rise early and meditate and then recite all the <a href="/wiki/Five_Banis" title="Five Banis">Five Banis</a> of Nitnem before breakfast.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Remembrance_of_the_divine_name">Remembrance of the divine name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Remembrance of the divine name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A key practice by Sikhs is remembrance<sup id="cite_ref-NaamSimran_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NaamSimran-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> of the Divine Name Vaheguru (Naam – the Name of the Lord).<sup id="cite_ref-Pruthi_2004_204_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pruthi_2004_204-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> This contemplation is done through <i><a href="/wiki/N%C4%81m_Jap%C5%8D" class="mw-redirect" title="Nām Japō">Nām Japna</a></i> (repetition of the divine name) or <i><a href="/wiki/Simran" title="Simran">Naam Simran</a></i> (remembrance of the divine Name through recitation).<sup id="cite_ref-NaamSimran_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NaamSimran-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The verbal repetition of the name of God or a sacred syllable has been an ancient established practice in religious traditions in India, however, Sikhism developed <i>Naam-simran</i> as an important Bhakti practice.<sup id="cite_ref-Dhillon1988_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dhillon1988-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Guru Nanak's ideal is the total exposure of one's being to the divine Name and a total conforming to <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">Dharma</a> or the "Divine Order". Nanak described the result of the disciplined application of <i>nām simraṇ</i> as a "growing towards and into God" through a gradual process of five stages. The last of these is <i><a href="/wiki/Sach_Khand" title="Sach Khand">sach khaṇḍ</a></i> (<i>The Realm of Truth</i>)&#160;&#8211;&#32;the final union of the spirit with God.<sup id="cite_ref-p254_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p254-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Service_and_action">Service and action</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Service and action">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Sikh Gurus taught that by constantly remembering the divine name (<i>naam simran</i>) and through selfless service, or <i>sēvā</i>, the devotee overcomes egoism (<i><a href="/wiki/Haumai" title="Haumai">Haumai</a></i>). This, it states, is the primary root of five evil impulses and the cycle of <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation#Sikhism" title="Reincarnation">rebirth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Service in Sikhism takes three forms: "Tan" – physical service; "Man" – mental service (such as studying to help others); and "Dhan" – material service.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Sikhism stresses <i><a href="/wiki/Kirat_kar%C5%8D" class="mw-redirect" title="Kirat karō">kirat karō</a></i>: that is "honest work". Sikh teachings also stress the concept of sharing, or <i><a href="/wiki/Va%E1%B9%87%E1%B8%8D_chakk%C5%8D" class="mw-redirect" title="Vaṇḍ chakkō">vaṇḍ chakkō</a></i>, giving to the needy for the benefit of the community.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Justice_and_equality">Justice and equality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Justice and equality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sikhism regards God as the true king, the king of all kings, the one who dispenses justice through the law of <i><a href="/wiki/Karma" title="Karma">karma</a></i>, a retributive model and divine grace.<sup id="cite_ref-Justice_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Justice-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chanchreek_2007_142_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chanchreek_2007_142-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dugga_2001_33_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dugga_2001_33-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term for justice in the Sikh tradition is "Niau".<sup id="cite_ref-Justice_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Justice-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> It is related to the term "<a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharam</a>" which in Sikhism connotes 'moral order' and righteousness.<sup id="cite_ref-Justice_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Justice-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> According to the Tenth Sikh Guru <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a>, states Pashaura Singh – a professor of Sikh Studies, "one must first try all the peaceful means of negotiation in the pursuit of justice" and if these fail then it is legitimate to "draw the sword in defense of righteousness".<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Sikhism considers "an attack on dharam is an attack on justice, on righteousness, and on the moral order generally" and the dharam "must be defended at all costs".<sup id="cite_ref-Mcleod_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mcleod-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The divine name is its antidote for all pain and vices. Forgiveness is taught as a virtue in Sikhism, yet it also teaches its faithful to shun those with evil intentions and to pick up the sword to fight injustice and religious persecution.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sikhism does not differentiate religious obligations by gender. God in Sikhism has no gender, and the Sikh scripture does not discriminate against women, nor bar them from any roles.<sup id="cite_ref-McLeod2009p70_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2009p70-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Women in Sikhism have led battles and issued <a href="/wiki/Hukamnama" title="Hukamnama">hukamnamas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McLeod2009p70_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2009p70-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ten_gurus_and_authority">Ten gurus and authority</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ten gurus and authority">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sikh_gurus" title="Sikh gurus">Sikh gurus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg/170px-Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg/255px-Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg/340px-Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg 2x" data-file-width="887" data-file-height="1172" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sikh_Gurus_with_Bhai_Bala_and_Bhai_Mardana.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A rare <a href="/wiki/Tanjore_painting" class="mw-redirect" title="Tanjore painting">Tanjore</a>-style painting from the late 19th century depicting the ten Sikh Gurus with <a href="/wiki/Bhai_Bala" title="Bhai Bala">Bhai Bala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bhai_Mardana" title="Bhai Mardana">Bhai Mardana</a></div></div></div>
<p>The term <a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">guru</a> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <i>gurū</i>, meaning teacher, guide, or mentor. The traditions and philosophy of Sikhism were established by ten gurus from 1469 to 1708.<sup id="cite_ref-Panjab_Publishers_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Panjab_Publishers-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nesbitt2005_a_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nesbitt2005_a-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Each guru added to and reinforced the message taught by the previous, resulting in the creation of the Sikh religion. Guru Nanak was the first guru and appointed a disciple as successor. <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a> was the final guru in human form. Before his death, Guru Gobind Singh decreed in 1708, that the Gurū Granth Sāhib would be the final and perpetual guru of the Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-granthfinalguru_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-granthfinalguru-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Guru Nanak stated that his Guru is God who is the same from the beginning of time to the end of time.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Nanak claimed to be God's mouthpiece, God's slave and servant, but maintained that he was only a guide and teacher.<sup id="cite_ref-ksingh116_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ksingh116-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Nanak stated that the human Guru is mortal, who is to be respected and loved but not worshipped.<sup id="cite_ref-ksingh116_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ksingh116-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> When Guru, or Satguru (The true guru) is used in <i>Gurbani</i> it is often referring to the highest expression of truthfulness – God.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Guru_Angad" title="Guru Angad">Guru Angad</a> succeeded Guru Nanak. Later, an important phase in the development of Sikhism came with the third successor, Guru Amar Das. Guru Nanak's teachings emphasised the pursuit of salvation; Guru Amar Das began building a cohesive community of followers with initiatives such as sanctioning distinctive ceremonies for birth, marriage, and death. Amar Das also established the <i>manji</i> (comparable to a <a href="/wiki/Diocese" title="Diocese">diocese</a>) system of clerical supervision.<sup id="cite_ref-p254_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p254-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Guru_Amar_Das" title="Guru Amar Das">Guru Amar Das</a>'s successor and son-in-law <a href="/wiki/Guru_Ram_Das" title="Guru Ram Das">Guru Ram Das</a> founded the city of <a href="/wiki/Amritsar" title="Amritsar">Amritsar</a>, which is home of the <a href="/wiki/Harimandir_Sahib" class="mw-redirect" title="Harimandir Sahib">Harimandir Sahib</a> and regarded widely as the holiest city for all Sikhs. <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> was arrested by <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal</a> authorities who were suspicious and hostile to the religious order he was developing.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> His persecution and death inspired his successors to promote a military and political organization of Sikh communities to defend themselves against the attacks of Mughal forces.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg/220px-Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg/330px-Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg/440px-Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1727" data-file-height="1188" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Interior_of_Akal_Takht.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The interior of the <a href="/wiki/Akal_Takht" title="Akal Takht">Akal Takht</a></div></div></div><p>The Sikh gurus established a mechanism which allowed the Sikh religion to react as a community to changing circumstances. The sixth guru, <a href="/wiki/Guru_Har_Gobind" class="mw-redirect" title="Guru Har Gobind">Guru Hargobind</a>, was responsible for the creation of the concept of <a href="/wiki/Akal_Takht" title="Akal Takht">Akal Takht</a> (<i>throne of the timeless one</i>), which serves as the supreme decision-making centre of Sikhism and sits opposite the <a href="/wiki/Harmandir_Sahib" class="mw-redirect" title="Harmandir Sahib">Harmandir Sahib</a>. The Akal Takht is located in the city of Amritsar. The leader is appointed by the Shiromani Gurdwara Pabandhak Committee (SPGC). The <i><a href="/wiki/Sarbat_Khalsa" title="Sarbat Khalsa">Sarbat Ḵẖālsā</a></i> (a representative portion of the Khalsa Panth) historically gathers at the Akal Takht on special festivals such as <a href="/wiki/Vaisakhi" title="Vaisakhi">Vaisakhi</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hola_Mohalla" title="Hola Mohalla">Hola Mohalla</a> and when there is a need to discuss matters that affect the entire Sikh nation. A <i><a href="/wiki/Gurmata" title="Gurmata">gurmatā</a></i> (literally, <i>guru's intention</i>) is an order passed by the Sarbat Ḵẖālsā in the presence of the Gurū Granth Sāhib. A gurmatā may only be passed on a subject that affects the fundamental principles of Sikh religion; it is binding upon all Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The term <i><a href="/wiki/Hukamnama" title="Hukamnama">hukamnāmā</a></i> (literally, <i>edict</i> or <i>royal order</i>) is often used interchangeably with the term gurmatā. However, a hukamnāmā formally refers to a hymn from the Gurū Granth Sāhib which is a given order to Sikhs.
</p><table class="wikitable collapsible collapsed">

<tbody><tr>
<th class="navbox-title">Chronology of the ten Sikh Gurus
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SikhGurus-LifeSpans-GurushipSpans.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Graph showing Life Spans and Guruship Spans of Sikh Gurus" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/SikhGurus-LifeSpans-GurushipSpans.jpg/800px-SikhGurus-LifeSpans-GurushipSpans.jpg" decoding="async" width="800" height="526" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/SikhGurus-LifeSpans-GurushipSpans.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="929" data-file-height="611" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SikhGurus-LifeSpans-GurushipSpans.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Approximate Life Spans and Guruship Spans of the 10 Sikh gurus</div></div></div></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The word Guru in Sikhism also refers to <i>Akal Purkh</i> (God), and God and Guru are often synonymous in <i><a href="/wiki/Gurbani" title="Gurbani">Gurbani</a></i> (Sikh writings).<sup id="cite_ref-Panjab_Publishers_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Panjab_Publishers-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scripture">Scripture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Scripture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There is one primary scripture for the Sikhs: the Gurū Granth Sāhib. It is sometimes synonymously referred to as the Ādi Granth.<sup id="cite_ref-shacklexvii_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexvii-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Chronologically, however, the Ādi Granth – literally, <i>The First Volume</i>, refers to the version of the scripture created by <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> in 1604.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The Gurū Granth Sāhib is the final expanded version of the scripture compiled by <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-shacklexvii_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexvii-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> While the Guru Granth Sahib is an unquestioned scripture in Sikhism, another important religious text, the <a href="/wiki/Dasam_Granth" title="Dasam Granth">Dasam Granth</a>, does not enjoy universal consensus, and is considered a secondary scripture by many Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-shacklexvii_117-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexvii-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adi_Granth">Adi Granth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Adi Granth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/%C4%80di_Granth" class="mw-redirect" title="Ādi Granth">Ādi Granth</a></div>
<p>The Ādi Granth was compiled primarily by <a href="/wiki/Bhai_Gurdas" title="Bhai Gurdas">Bhai Gurdas</a> under the supervision of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> between the years 1603 and 1604.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> It is written in the <a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi" title="Gurmukhi">Gurmukhī</a> script, which is a descendant of the <a href="/wiki/La%E1%B9%87%E1%B8%8D%C4%81" class="mw-redirect" title="Laṇḍā">Laṇḍā</a> script used in the Punjab at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi" title="Gurmukhi">Gurmukhī</a> script was standardised by Guru Angad, the second guru of the Sikhs, for use in the Sikh scriptures and is thought to have been influenced by the <a href="/wiki/%C5%9A%C4%81rad%C4%81_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Śāradā script">Śāradā</a> and <a href="/wiki/Devan%C4%81gar%C4%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Devanāgarī">Devanāgarī</a> scripts. An authoritative scripture was created to protect the integrity of hymns and teachings of the Sikh gurus, and thirteen Hindu and two Muslim bhagats of the <a href="/wiki/Bhakti_movement" title="Bhakti movement">Bhakti movement</a> sant tradition in medieval India.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The thirteen Hindu bhagats whose teachings were entered into the text included <a href="/wiki/Ramananda" title="Ramananda">Ramananda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Namdev" title="Namdev">Namdev</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Pipa" title="Bhagat Pipa">Pipa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ravidas" title="Ravidas">Ravidas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Beni" title="Bhagat Beni">Beni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Bhikhan" title="Bhagat Bhikhan">Bhikhan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Dhanna" title="Bhagat Dhanna">Dhanna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jayadeva" title="Jayadeva">Jaidev</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Parmanand" title="Bhagat Parmanand">Parmanand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Sadhana" title="Bhagat Sadhana">Sadhana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Sain" title="Bhagat Sain">Sain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sur_(poet)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sur (poet)">Sur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhagat_Trilochan" title="Bhagat Trilochan">Trilochan</a>, while the two Muslim bhagats were <a href="/wiki/Kabir" title="Kabir">Kabir</a> and Sufi saint <a href="/wiki/Fariduddin_Ganjshakar" title="Fariduddin Ganjshakar">Farid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-shapiro924_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro924-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Guru_Granth_Sahib">Guru Granth Sahib</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Guru Granth Sahib">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg/170px-Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="112" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg/255px-Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg/340px-Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="847" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Guru_Granth_Sahib.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gurū Granth Sāhib – the primary scripture of Sikhism</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gur%C5%AB_Granth_S%C4%81hib" class="mw-redirect" title="Gurū Granth Sāhib">Gurū Granth Sāhib</a></div>
<p>The Guru Granth Sahib is the holy scripture of the Sikhs, and regarded as the living Guru.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Compilation">Compilation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Compilation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Guru Granth started as a volume of Guru Nanak's poetic compositions. Prior to his death, he passed on his volume to Guru Angad (Guru 1539–1551). The final version of the <a href="/wiki/Gur%C5%AB_Granth_S%C4%81hib" class="mw-redirect" title="Gurū Granth Sāhib">Gurū Granth Sāhib</a> was compiled by <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a> in 1678. It consists of the original Ādi Granth with the addition of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Tegh_Bahadur" title="Guru Tegh Bahadur">Guru Tegh Bahadur</a>'s hymns. The predominant bulk of Guru Granth Sahib is compositions by seven Sikh Gurus – Guru Nanak, Guru Angad, Guru Amar Das, Guru Ram Das, Guru Arjan, Guru Teg Bahadur and Guru Gobind Singh. It also contains the traditions and teachings of thirteen <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> Bhakti movement <i>sants</i> (saints) such as <a href="/wiki/Ramananda" title="Ramananda">Ramananda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Namdev" title="Namdev">Namdev</a> among others, and two <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> saints namely <a href="/wiki/Kabir" title="Kabir">Kabir</a> and the Sufi <a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Farid" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheikh Farid">Sheikh Farid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-shapiro924_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro924-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-p254_53-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p254-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The text comprises 6,000 <i><a href="/wiki/Shabhad" class="mw-redirect" title="Shabhad">śabads</a></i> (line compositions),<sup id="cite_ref-shacklexvii_117-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexvii-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> which are poetically rendered and set to rhythmic ancient north Indian classical music.<sup id="cite_ref-king359_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-king359-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The bulk of the scripture is classified into thirty one <i><a href="/wiki/Raga" title="Raga">rāgas</a></i>, with each <a href="/wiki/Ragas_in_the_Guru_Granth_Sahib" title="Ragas in the Guru Granth Sahib">Granth rāga</a> subdivided according to length and author. The hymns in the scripture are arranged primarily by the <i>rāgas</i> in which they are read.<sup id="cite_ref-shacklexvii_117-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexvii-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_and_script">Language and script</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Language and script">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The main language used in the scripture is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Sant_Bhasha" title="Sant Bhasha">Sant Bhāṣā</a></i>, a language related to both Punjabi and <a href="/wiki/Hindi_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindi language">Hindi</a> and used extensively across medieval northern India by proponents of popular devotional religion (<a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">bhakti</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-parrinderp259_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parrinderp259-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The text is printed in Gurumukhi script, believed to have been developed by Guru Angad,<sup id="cite_ref-shacklexvii_117-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexvii-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> but it shares the Indo-European roots found in numerous regional languages of India.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Teachings">Teachings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Teachings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sikh_musicians.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Sikh_musicians.jpg/170px-Sikh_musicians.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Sikh_musicians.jpg/255px-Sikh_musicians.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Sikh_musicians.jpg/340px-Sikh_musicians.jpg 2x" data-file-width="514" data-file-height="599" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sikh_musicians.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A group of <a href="/wiki/Sikh_music" title="Sikh music">Sikh musicians</a> at the Golden Temple complex</div></div></div>
<p>The vision in the Guru Granth Sahib, states Torkel Brekke, is a society based on divine justice without oppression of any kind.<sup id="cite_ref-brekke673_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brekke673-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Granth begins with the <i><a href="/wiki/Mul_Mantra" class="mw-redirect" title="Mul Mantra">Mūl Mantra</a></i>, an iconic verse which received Guru Nanak directly from Akal Purakh (God).
The traditional Mul Mantar goes from Ik Oankar until Nanak Hosee Bhee Sach.
</p>
<dl><dd>One God exists, truth by name, creative power, without fear, without enmity, timeless form, unborn, self-existent, by the Guru's grace.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dd>(<a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a>: <span lang="pa">ੴ ਸਤਿ ਨਾਮੁ ਕਰਤਾ ਪੁਰਖੁ ਨਿਰਭਉ ਨਿਰਵੈਰੁ ਅਕਾਲ ਮੂਰਤਿ ਅਜੂਨੀ ਸੈਭੰ ਗੁਰ ਪ੍ਰਸਾਦਿ ॥</span>, <small>romanized:&#160;</small><i lang="pa-Latn" title="Punjabi-language romanization">Ika ōaṅkāra sati nāmu karatā purakhu nirabha'u niravairu akāla mūrati ajūnī saibhaṅ gura prasādi</i>)</dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="As_Guru">As Guru</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: As Guru">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Tenth Guru, Guru Gobind Singh ji, named the Sikh scripture Guru Granth Sahib as his successor, terminating the line of human Gurus and making the scripture the literal embodiment of the eternal, impersonal Guru, where Gods/Gurus word serves as the spiritual guide for Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-WH_McLeod_2014_page_17_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WH_McLeod_2014_page_17-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-William_James_2011_pages_241-242_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-William_James_2011_pages_241-242-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-granthfinalguru_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-granthfinalguru-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>All Sikhs are commanded to take the Granth as Guru</dd>
<dd>(<a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a>: <span lang="pa">ਸੱਬ ਸਿੱਖਣ ਕੋ ਹੁਕਮ ਹੈ ਗੁਰੂ ਮਾਨਯੋ ਗ੍ਰੰਥ ।</span>, <small>romanized:&#160;</small><i lang="pa-Latn" title="Punjabi-language romanization">Sabb sikkhaṇ kō hukam hai <a href="/wiki/Guru_Maneyo_Granth" title="Guru Maneyo Granth">gurū mānyō granth</a></i>)</dd></dl>
<p>The Guru Granth Sahib is installed in Sikh <i>Gurdwara</i> (temple); many Sikhs bow or prostrate before it on entering the temple. The Guru Granth Sahib is installed every morning and put to bed at night in many <i>Gurdwaras</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The Granth is revered as eternal <i><a href="/wiki/Gurbani" title="Gurbani">gurbānī</a></i> and the spiritual authority.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The copies of the Guru Granth Sahib are not regarded as material objects, but as living subjects which are alive.<sup id="cite_ref-Myrvold_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myrvold-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> According to Myrvold, the Sikh scripture is treated with respect like a living person, in a manner similar to the Gospel in early Christian worship. Old copies of the Sikh scripture are not thrown away, rather funerary services are performed.<sup id="cite_ref-Myrvold_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myrvold-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In India the Guru Granth Sahib is even officially recognised by the Supreme Court of India as a judicial person which can receive donations and own land.<sup id="cite_ref-Myrvold_134-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myrvold-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Yet, some Sikhs also warn that, without true comprehension of the text, veneration for the text can lead to <a href="/wiki/Bibliolatry" title="Bibliolatry">bibliolatry</a>, with the concrete form of the teachings becoming the object of worship instead of the teachings themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-Myrvold_134-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myrvold-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_Hinduism_and_Islam">Relation to Hinduism and Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Relation to Hinduism and Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sikhism_and_Hinduism" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikhism and Hinduism">Sikhism and Hinduism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam_and_Sikhism" title="Islam and Sikhism">Islam and Sikhism</a></div> 
<p>The Sikh scriptures use <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> terminology, with references to the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a>, and the names of gods and goddesses in Hindu bhakti movement traditions, such as Vishnu, Shiva, Brahma, Parvati, Lakshmi, Saraswati, Rama, Krishna, but not to worship.<sup id="cite_ref-brekke673_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brekke673-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (December 2017)">self-published source</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> It also refers to the spiritual concepts in Hinduism (<i>Ishvara, Bhagavan, Brahman</i>) and the concept of God in <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> (<i>Allah</i>) to assert that these are just "alternate names for the Almighty One".<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While the Guru Granth Sahib acknowledges the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qur%27an" class="mw-redirect" title="Qur&#39;an">Qur'an</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> it does not imply a <a href="/wiki/Wiktionary" title="Wiktionary">syncretic</a> bridge between Hinduism and Islam,<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> but emphasises focusing on nitnem banis like <a href="/wiki/Japji_Sahib" title="Japji Sahib">Japu</a> (repeating <a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantra</a> of the divine Name of God – <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vaheguru&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Vaheguru (page does not exist)">Vaheguru</a>), instead of Muslim practices such as circumcision or praying on a carpet, or Hindu rituals such as wearing thread or praying in a river.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dasam_Granth">Dasam Granth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Dasam Granth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg/220px-Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg/330px-Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg/440px-Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3582" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dasam.Granth.Frontispiece.BL.Manuscript.1825-1850.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Dasam Granth is a Sikh scripture which contains texts attributed to Guru Gobind Singh. The major narrative in the text is on <a href="/wiki/Chaubis_Avtar" title="Chaubis Avtar">Chaubis Avtar</a> (24 <a href="/wiki/Avatars" class="mw-redirect" title="Avatars">Avatars</a> of Hindu god <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>), <a href="/wiki/Rudra" title="Rudra">Rudra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a>, the Hindu warrior goddess <a href="/wiki/Chandi" title="Chandi">Chandi</a> and a story of <a href="/wiki/Rama" title="Rama">Rama</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bachittar_Natak" title="Bachittar Natak">Bachittar Natak</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jdeol31_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jdeol31-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dasam_Granth" title="Dasam Granth">Dasam Granth</a></div>
<p>The Dasam Granth is a scripture of Sikhs which contains texts attributed to the Guru Gobind Singh. The <i>Dasam Granth</i> is important to a great number of Sikhs, however it does not have the same authority as the <i>Guru Granth Sahib</i>. Some compositions of the <i>Dasam Granth</i> like <a href="/wiki/Jaap_Sahib" title="Jaap Sahib">Jaap Sahib</a>, (<a href="/wiki/Amrit_Savaiye" class="mw-redirect" title="Amrit Savaiye">Amrit Savaiye</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Chaupai_(Sikhism)" title="Chaupai (Sikhism)">Benti Chaupai</a> are part of the daily prayers (<a href="/wiki/Nitnem" title="Nitnem">Nitnem</a>) for Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Dasam Granth</i> is largely versions of Hindu mythology from the <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a>, secular stories from a variety of sources called <i>Charitro Pakhyan</i> – tales to protect careless men from perils of lust.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Five versions of <i>Dasam Granth</i> exist, and the authenticity of the <i>Dasam Granth</i> is amongst the most debated topics within Sikhism. The text played a significant role in Sikh history, but in modern times parts of the text have seen antipathy and discussion among Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-jdeol31_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jdeol31-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Janamsakhis">Janamsakhis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Janamsakhis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Janams%C4%81kh%C4%ABs" class="mw-redirect" title="Janamsākhīs">Janamsākhīs</a></div>
<p>The Janamsākhīs (literally <i>birth stories</i>), are writings which profess to be biographies of Nanak. Although not scripture in the strictest sense, they provide a <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiographic</a> look at Nanak's life and the early start of Sikhism. There are several – often contradictory and sometimes unreliable – Janamsākhīs and they are not held in the same regard as other sources of scriptural knowledge.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observances">Observances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Observances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg/220px-InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg/330px-InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg/440px-InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:InsideSikhGurdwara.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Darbar_Sahib_Hall" title="Darbar Sahib Hall">Darbar Sahib</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Gurdwara" title="Gurdwara">Gurdwara</a></div></div></div>
<p>Observant Sikhs adhere to long-standing practices and traditions to strengthen and express their faith. The daily recitation of the divine name of God Vaheguru and from memory of specific passages from the Gurū Granth Sāhib, like the <i>Japu</i> (or <i>Japjī</i>, literally <i>chant</i>) hymns is recommended immediately after rising and bathing. Baptized Sikhs recite the five morning prayers, the evening and night prayer. Family customs include both reading passages from the scripture and attending the <a href="/wiki/Gurudwara" class="mw-redirect" title="Gurudwara">gurdwara</a> (also <i>gurduārā</i>, meaning <i>the doorway to God</i>; sometimes transliterated as <i>gurudwara</i>). There are many gurdwaras prominently constructed and maintained across India, as well as in almost every nation where Sikhs reside. Gurdwaras are open to all, regardless of religion, background, caste, or race.
</p><p>Worship in a gurdwara consists chiefly of singing of passages from the scripture. Sikhs will commonly enter the gurdwara, touch the ground before the holy scripture with their foreheads. The recitation of the eighteenth century <i><a href="/wiki/Ard%C4%81s" title="Ardās">ardās</a></i> is also customary for attending Sikhs. The ardās recalls past sufferings and glories of the community, invoking divine grace for all humanity.<sup id="cite_ref-p260_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p260-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The gurdwara is also the location for the historic Sikh practice of "<a href="/wiki/Langar_(Sikhism)" title="Langar (Sikhism)">Langar</a>" or the community meal. All gurdwaras are open to anyone of any faith for a free meal, always vegetarian.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> People eat together, and the kitchen is maintained and serviced by Sikh community volunteers.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
