<h3><span id="Sikh_festivals.2Fevents"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikh_festivals/events">Sikh festivals/events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Sikh festivals/events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Guru_Amar_Das" title="Guru Amar Das">Guru Amar Das</a> chose festivals for celebration by Sikhs like <a href="/wiki/Vaisakhi" title="Vaisakhi">Vaisakhi</a>, wherein he asked Sikhs to assemble and share the festivities as a community.<sup id="cite_ref-ColeSambhi1995p135_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ColeSambhi1995p135-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Vaisakhi is one of the most important festivals of Sikhs, while other significant festivals commemorate the birth, lives of the Gurus and Sikh martyrs. Historically, these festivals have been based on the moon calendar <a href="/wiki/Bikrami_calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Bikrami calendar">Bikrami calendar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nesbittbc_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nesbittbc-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> In 2003, the <a href="/wiki/SGPC" class="mw-redirect" title="SGPC">SGPC</a>, the Sikh organisation in charge of upkeep of the historical gurdwaras of Punjab, adopted <a href="/wiki/Nanakshahi" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanakshahi">Nanakshahi</a> calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-nesbittbc_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nesbittbc-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> The new calendar is highly controversial among Sikhs and is not universally accepted. Sikh festivals include the following:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Vaisakhi" title="Vaisakhi">Vaisakhi</a> which includes Parades and Nagar Kirtan occurs on 13 April or 14 April. Sikhs celebrate it because on this day which fell on 30 March 1699, the tenth Guru, Gobind Singh, inaugurated the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a>, the 11th body of Guru Granth Sahib and leader of Sikhs till eternity.
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Nagar_Kirtan" title="Nagar Kirtan">Nagar Kirtan</a> involves the processional singing of holy hymns throughout a community. While practiced at any time, it is customary in the month of Visakhi (or Vaisakhi). Traditionally, the procession is led by the saffron-robed Panj Piare (the five beloved of the Guru), who are followed by the Guru Granth Sahib, the holy Sikh scripture, which is placed on a float.</li></ul></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin:0 auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:768px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:Yuba_City_Nagar_Kirtan_2011.jpg" class="image" title="Nagar Kirtan crowd listening to Kirtan at Yuba City, California."><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Yuba_City_Nagar_Kirtan_2011.jpg/760px-Yuba_City_Nagar_Kirtan_2011.jpg" decoding="async" width="760" height="240" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Yuba_City_Nagar_Kirtan_2011.jpg/1140px-Yuba_City_Nagar_Kirtan_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/Yuba_City_Nagar_Kirtan_2011.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1276" data-file-height="403" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yuba_City_Nagar_Kirtan_2011.jpg" title="File:Yuba City Nagar Kirtan 2011.jpg"> </a></div><a href="/wiki/Nagar_Kirtan" title="Nagar Kirtan">Nagar Kirtan</a> crowd listening to <a href="/wiki/Kirtan" title="Kirtan">Kirtan</a> at <a href="/wiki/Yuba_City,_California" title="Yuba City, California">Yuba City, California</a>.</div></div></div>
<ul><li>Band Chor Diwas has been another important Sikh festival in its history.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> In recent years, instead of Diwali, the post-2003 calendar released by SGPC has named it the <a href="/wiki/Bandi_Chhor_Divas" title="Bandi Chhor Divas">Bandi Chhor</a> divas.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Sikhs celebrate <a href="/wiki/Guru_Hargobind" title="Guru Hargobind">Guru Hargobind</a>'s release from the <a href="/wiki/Gwalior_Fort" title="Gwalior Fort">Gwalior Fort</a>, with several innocent Raja kings who were also imprisoned by Mughal Emperor <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a> in 1619. This day continues to be commemorated on the same day of Hindu festival of <a href="/wiki/Diwali" title="Diwali">Diwali</a>, with lights, fireworks and festivities.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hola_Mohalla" title="Hola Mohalla">Hola Mohalla</a> is a tradition started by Guru Gobind Singh. It starts the day after Sikhs celebrate <a href="/wiki/Holi" title="Holi">Holi</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-McLeod2009p95_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2009p95-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> sometimes referred to as <i>Hola</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Roy2005p192_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roy2005p192-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Guru Gobind Singh modified Holi with a three-day <a href="/wiki/Hola_Mohalla" title="Hola Mohalla">Hola Mohalla</a> extension festival of martial arts. The extension started the day after the Holi festival in <a href="/wiki/Anandpur_Sahib" title="Anandpur Sahib">Anandpur Sahib</a>, where Sikh soldiers would train in mock battles, compete in horsemanship, athletics, archery and military exercises.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gurpurb" title="Gurpurb">Gurpurbs</a> are celebrations or commemorations based on the lives of the Sikh gurus. They tend to be either birthdays or celebrations of Sikh martyrdom. All ten Gurus have Gurpurbs on the Nanakshahi calendar, but it is Guru Nanak and Guru Gobind Singh who have a gurpurb that is widely celebrated in Gurdwaras and Sikh homes. The martyrdoms are also known as a Shaheedi Gurpurbs, which mark the martyrdom anniversary of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guru_Tegh_Bahadur" title="Guru Tegh Bahadur">Guru Tegh Bahadur</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ceremonies_and_customs">Ceremonies and customs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Ceremonies and customs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sikh_wedding.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sikh_wedding.jpg/220px-Sikh_wedding.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sikh_wedding.jpg/330px-Sikh_wedding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sikh_wedding.jpg/440px-Sikh_wedding.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1728" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sikh_wedding.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sikh_wedding" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikh wedding">Sikh wedding</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi,_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi%2C_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg/220px-Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi%2C_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi%2C_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg/330px-Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi%2C_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi%2C_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg/440px-Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi%2C_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sikh_funeral_procession._Mandi,_Himachel_Pradesh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sikh funeral procession, <a href="/wiki/Mandi,_Himachal_Pradesh" title="Mandi, Himachal Pradesh">Mandi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Himachal_Pradesh" title="Himachal Pradesh">Himachal Pradesh</a></div></div></div>
<p>Khalsa Sikhs have also supported and helped develop major pilgrimage traditions to sacred sites such as Harmandir Sahib, Anandpur Sahib, Fatehgarh Sahib, Patna Sahib, Hazur Nanded Sahib, Hemkund Sahib and others.<sup id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994p68_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994p68-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Sikh pilgrims and Sikhs of other sects customarily consider these as holy and a part of their <i>Tirath</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferrari2011p48_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferrari2011p48-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hola_Mohalla" title="Hola Mohalla">Hola Mohalla</a> around the festival of <a href="/wiki/Holi" title="Holi">Holi</a>, for example, is a ceremonial and customary gathering every year in <a href="/wiki/Anandpur_Sahib" title="Anandpur Sahib">Anandpur Sahib</a> attracting over 100,000 Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Major Sikh temples feature a <i>sarovar</i> where some Sikhs take a customary dip. Some take home the sacred water of the tank particularly for sick friends and relatives,<sup id="cite_ref-singha7_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-singha7-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> believing that the waters of such sacred sites have restorative powers and the ability to purify one's <i>karma</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Upon a child's birth, the Guru Granth Sahib is opened at a random point and the child is named using the first letter on the top left hand corner of the left page. All boys are given the last name <a href="/wiki/Singh" title="Singh">Singh</a>, and all girls are given the last name <a href="/wiki/Kaur" title="Kaur">Kaur</a> (this was once a title which was conferred on an individual upon joining the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sikh marriage ritual includes the <i><a href="/wiki/Anand_Karaj" title="Anand Karaj">anand kāraj</a></i> ceremony.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ruether2006p700_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ruether2006p700-168">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> The marriage ceremony is performed in front of the Guru Granth Sahib by a baptized Khalsa, Granthi of the Gurdwara.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fenech33_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fenech33-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The tradition of circling the Guru Granth Sahib and Anand Karaj among Khalsa is practised since the fourth Guru, Guru Ram Das. Its official recognition and adoption came in 1909, during the <a href="/wiki/Singh_Sabha_Movement" title="Singh Sabha Movement">Singh Sabha Movement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-fenech33_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fenech33-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Upon death, the body of a Sikh is usually cremated. If this is not possible, any respectful means of disposing the body may be employed. The <i>kīrtan sōhilā</i> and <i>ardās</i> prayers are performed during the funeral ceremony (known as <i><a href="/wiki/Antam_Sanskar" title="Antam Sanskar">antim sanskār</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initiation_and_the_Khalsa">Initiation and the Khalsa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Initiation and the Khalsa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a> (meaning "Sovereign") is the collective name given by Guru Gobind Singh to those Sikhs who have been fully initiated by taking part in a ceremony called <i><a href="/wiki/Ammrit_sa%C3%B1c%C4%81r" class="mw-redirect" title="Ammrit sañcār">ammrit sañcār</a></i> (nectar ceremony).<sup id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p23_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p23-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> During this ceremony, sweetened water is stirred with a double-edged sword while liturgical prayers are sung; it is offered to the initiating Sikh, who ritually drinks it.<sup id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p23_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p23-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Many Sikhs are not formally and fully initiated, as they do not undergo this ceremony, but do adhere to some components of Sikhism and identify as Sikhs. The initiated Sikh, who is believed to be <a href="/wiki/Dvija#The_meaning_of_the_two_births" title="Dvija">reborn</a>, is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Amritdhari" class="mw-redirect" title="Amritdhari">Amritdhari</a> or Khalsa Sikh, while those who are not initiated or baptised are referred to as Kesdhari or Sahajdhari Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p23_172-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p23-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first time that this ceremony took place was on <a href="/wiki/Vaisakhi" title="Vaisakhi">Vaisakhi</a>, which fell on 30 March 1699 at <a href="/wiki/Anandpur_Sahib" title="Anandpur Sahib">Anandpur Sahib</a> in Punjab.<sup id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p23_172-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p23-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> It was on that occasion that Gobind Singh baptised the <a href="/wiki/Panj_Piare" class="mw-redirect" title="Panj Piare">Pañj Piārē</a> – the five beloved ones, who in turn baptised Guru Gobind Singh himself. To males who initiated, the last name Singh, meaning "lion", was given, while the last name Kaur, meaning "princess", was given to baptised Sikh females.<sup id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p23_172-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p23-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Baptised <a href="/wiki/Sikhs" title="Sikhs">Sikhs</a> wear five items, called the <a href="/wiki/Five_Ks" class="mw-redirect" title="Five Ks">Five Ks</a> (in Punjabi known as <i>pañj kakkē</i> or <i>pañj kakār</i>), at all times. The five items are: <i><a href="/wiki/Kesh_(Sikhism)" title="Kesh (Sikhism)">kēs</a></i> (uncut hair), <i><a href="/wiki/Kanga_(Sikhism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kanga (Sikhism)">kaṅghā</a></i> (small wooden comb), <i><a href="/wiki/Kara_(Sikhism)" title="Kara (Sikhism)">kaṛā</a></i> (circular steel or iron bracelet), <i><a href="/wiki/Kirpan" title="Kirpan">kirpān</a></i> (sword/dagger), and <i><a href="/wiki/Kaccha" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaccha">kacchera</a></i> (special undergarment).<sup id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p23_172-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p23-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> The Five Ks have both practical and symbolic purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sikhism" title="History of Sikhism">History of Sikhism</a></div>
<p>Guru Nanak (1469–1539), the founder of Sikhism, was born in the village of <i>Rāi Bhōi dī Talwandī</i>, now called <a href="/wiki/Nankana_Sahib" title="Nankana Sahib">Nankana Sahib</a> (in present-day <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> His parents were <a href="/wiki/Khatri" title="Khatri">Khatri</a> <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiography</a> <i>Puratan Janamsakhi</i> composed more than two centuries after his death and probably based on <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral tradition</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Nanak as a boy was fascinated by religion and spiritual matters, spending time with wandering ascetics and holy men.<sup id="cite_ref-shacklexiii_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexiii-179">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> His friend was Mardana, a Muslim. Together they would sing devotional songs all night in front of the public, and bathe in the river in the morning. One day, at the usual bath, Nanak went missing and his family feared he had drowned. Three days later he returned home, and declared: "There is no Hindu, there is no Muslim" ("<i>nā kōi hindū nā kōi musalmān</i>"). Thereafter, Nanak started preaching his ideas that form the tenets of Sikhism. In 1526, Guru Nanak at age 50, started a small commune in Kartarpur and his disciples came to be known as <i>Sikhs</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-shacklexiii_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shacklexiii-179">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Although the exact account of his itinerary is disputed, hagiographic accounts state he made five major journeys, spanning thousands of miles, the first tour being east towards <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a>, the second south towards <a href="/wiki/Andhra_Pradesh" title="Andhra Pradesh">Andhra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a>, the third north to <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ladakh" title="Ladakh">Ladakh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Meru" title="Mount Meru">Mount Sumeru</a><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>, and the fourth to <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> In his last and final tour, he returned to the banks of the Ravi River to end his days.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are two competing theories on Guru Nanak's teachings.<sup id="cite_ref-arvindmandair131_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arvindmandair131-183">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> One, according to Cole and Sambhi, is based on hagiographical <a href="/wiki/Janamsakhis" title="Janamsakhis">Janamsakhis</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> and states that Nanak's teachings and Sikhism were a revelation from God, and not a social protest movement nor any attempt to reconcile Hinduism and Islam in the 15th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Cole_Sambhi_Dic_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cole_Sambhi_Dic-185">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> The other states, Nanak was a <a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">Guru</a>. According to Singha, "Sikhism does not subscribe to the theory of incarnation or the concept of prophethood. But it has a pivotal concept of Guru. He is not an incarnation of God, not even a prophet. He is an illumined soul."<sup id="cite_ref-HS_Singha_2009_page_104_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HS_Singha_2009_page_104-186">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> The hagiographical <i>Janamsakhis</i> were not written by Nanak, but by later followers without regard for historical accuracy, and contain numerous legends and myths created to show respect for Nanak.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> The term revelation, clarify Cole and Sambhi, in Sikhism is not limited to the teachings of Nanak, they include all Sikh Gurus, as well as the words of past, present and future men and women, who possess divine knowledge intuitively through meditation. The Sikh revelations include the words of non-Sikh <a href="/wiki/Bhagat" title="Bhagat">bhagats</a>, some who lived and died before the birth of Nanak, and whose teachings are part of the Sikh scriptures.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> The Adi Granth and successive Sikh Gurus repeatedly emphasised, states Mandair, that Sikhism is "not about hearing voices from God, but it is about changing the nature of the human mind, and anyone can achieve direct experience and spiritual perfection at any time".<sup id="cite_ref-arvindmandair131_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arvindmandair131-183">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The roots of the Sikh tradition are, states Louis Fenech, perhaps in the <a href="/wiki/Sant_(religion)" title="Sant (religion)">Sant</a>-tradition of India whose ideology grew to become the Bhakti tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-Louis_Fenech_2014_page_35_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Louis_Fenech_2014_page_35-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, adds Fenech, "Indic mythology permeates the Sikh sacred canon, the <i>Guru Granth Sahib</i> and the secondary canon, the <i>Dasam Granth</i> and adds delicate nuance and substance to the sacred symbolic universe of the Sikhs of today and of their past ancestors".<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_influences">Historical influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Historical influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The development of Sikhism was influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Bhakti_movement" title="Bhakti movement">Bhakti movement</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-David_Lorenzen_1995_pages_1-2_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_Lorenzen_1995_pages_1-2-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Louis_Fenech_2014_page_35_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Louis_Fenech_2014_page_35-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-encyclobritannicasikh_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-encyclobritannicasikh-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnawa</a> Hinduism.<sup id="cite_ref-Kitagawa2013_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kitagawa2013-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> however, Sikhism was not simply an extension of the Bhakti movement.<sup id="cite_ref-Singha_67-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singha-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pruthi_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pruthi-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Sikhism developed while the region was being ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a>. Two of the Sikh gurus – <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guru_Tegh_Bahadur" title="Guru Tegh Bahadur">Guru Tegh Bahadur</a>, after they refused to convert to Islam, were tortured and executed by the Mughal rulers.<sup id="cite_ref-pashauraarjan_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pashauraarjan-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> The Islamic era persecution of Sikhs triggered the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a>, as an order for freedom of conscience and religion.<sup id="cite_ref-pashauraarjan_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pashauraarjan-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gandhi_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gandhi-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> A Sikh is expected to embody the qualities of a "Sant-Sipāhī"&#160;&#8211;&#32; a <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">saint-soldier</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chanchreek_2007_142_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chanchreek_2007_142-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dugga_2001_33_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dugga_2001_33-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_of_Sikhism">Growth of Sikhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Growth of Sikhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG/220px-Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG/330px-Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG/440px-Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG 2x" data-file-width="777" data-file-height="777" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Guru.Nanak.with.Hindu.holymen-b.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a> explaining <a href="/wiki/Sikh_beliefs" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikh beliefs">Sikh teachings</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sadhu" title="Sadhu">Sadhus</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1539, Guru Nanak chose his disciple <i>Lahiṇā</i> as a successor to the guruship rather than either of his sons. Lahiṇā was named <a href="/wiki/Guru_Angad" title="Guru Angad">Guru Angad</a> and became the second guru of the Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fenech2014p36_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fenech2014p36-194">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Nanak conferred his choice at the town of <a href="/wiki/Kartarpur_(Pakistan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kartarpur (Pakistan)">Kartarpur</a> on the banks of the river <a href="/wiki/Ravi_River" title="Ravi River">Ravi</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sri_Chand" title="Sri Chand">Sri Chand</a>, Guru Nanak's son was also a religious man, and continued his own commune of Sikhs. His followers came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Udasi" title="Udasi">Udasi</a> Sikhs, the first parallel sect of Sikhism that formed in Sikh history.<sup id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994p78_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994p78-195">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> The Udasis believe that the Guruship should have gone to Sri Chand, since he was a man of pious habits in addition to being Nanak's son.<sup id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994p78_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994p78-195">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Guru Angad, before joining Guru Nanak's commune, worked as a <i><a href="/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism)" title="Puja (Hinduism)">pujari</a></i> (priest) and religious teacher centered around Hindu goddess <a href="/wiki/Durga" title="Durga">Durga</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fenech2014p36_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fenech2014p36-194">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ColeSambhi1995p18_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ColeSambhi1995p18-196">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> On Nanak's advice, Guru Angad moved from Kartarpur to Khadur, where his wife <a href="/wiki/Mata_Khivi" class="mw-redirect" title="Mata Khivi">Khivi</a> and children were living, until he was able to bridge the divide between his followers and the Udasis. Guru Angad continued the work started by Guru Nanak and is widely credited for standardising the <a href="/wiki/Gurmukh%C4%AB_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Gurmukhī script">Gurmukhī script</a> as used in the sacred scripture of the Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-ColeSambhi1995p18_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ColeSambhi1995p18-196">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Guru_Amar_Das" title="Guru Amar Das">Guru Amar Das</a> became the third Sikh guru in 1552 at the age of 73. He adhered to the <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnavism</a> tradition of Hinduism for much of his life, before joining the commune of Guru Angad.<sup id="cite_ref-FenechMcLeod2014p29_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FenechMcLeod2014p29-197">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Goindval" title="Goindval">Goindval</a> became an important centre for Sikhism during the guruship of Guru Amar Das. He was a reformer, and discouraged veiling of women's faces (a Muslim custom) as well as <a href="/wiki/Sati_(practice)" title="Sati (practice)">sati</a> (a Hindu custom).<sup id="cite_ref-eosamardas_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eosamardas-199">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> He encouraged the <a href="/wiki/Kshatriya" title="Kshatriya">Kshatriya</a> people to fight in order to protect people and for the sake of justice, stating this is <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">Dharma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sambhi2005p29_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sambhi2005p29-201">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Guru Amar Das started the tradition of appointing <i>manji</i> (zones of religious administration with an appointed chief called <i>sangatias</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-FenechMcLeod2014p29_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FenechMcLeod2014p29-197">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> introduced the <i>dasvandh</i> ("the tenth" of income) system of revenue collection in the name of Guru and as pooled community religious resource,<sup id="cite_ref-Farhadian2015p342_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Farhadian2015p342-202">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> and the famed <i><a href="/wiki/Langar_(Sikhism)" title="Langar (Sikhism)">langar</a></i> tradition of Sikhism where anyone, without discrimination of any kind, could get a free meal in a communal seating. The collection of revenue from Sikhs through regional appointees helped Sikhism grow.<sup id="cite_ref-FenechMcLeod2014p29_197-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FenechMcLeod2014p29-197">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HaarKalsi2009p21_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HaarKalsi2009p21-203">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Guru Amar Das named his disciple and son-in-law <i>Jēṭhā</i> as the next Guru, who came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Guru_Ram_Das" title="Guru Ram Das">Guru Ram Das</a>. The new Guru faced hostilities from the sons of Guru Amar Das and therefore shifted his official base to lands identified by Guru Amar Das as Guru-ka-Chak.<sup id="cite_ref-Mandair2013p38_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair2013p38-204">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> He moved his commune of Sikhs there and the place then was called Ramdaspur, after him. This city grew and later became <a href="/wiki/Amritsar" title="Amritsar">Amritsar</a> – the holiest city of Sikhism.<sup id="cite_ref-McLeod1990p28_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod1990p28-205">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Guru Ram Das expanded the <i>manji</i> organization for clerical appointments in Sikh temples, and for revenue collections to theologically and economically support the Sikh movement.<sup id="cite_ref-Mandair2013p38_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair2013p38-204">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1581, <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> – youngest son of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Ram_Das" title="Guru Ram Das">Guru Ram Das</a>, became the fifth guru of the Sikhs. The choice of successor, as throughout most of the history of Sikh Guru successions, led to disputes and internal divisions among the Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-ShackleMandair2013xv_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ShackleMandair2013xv-206">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> The elder son of Guru Ram Das named <a href="/w/index.php?title=Prithi_Chand&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Prithi Chand (page does not exist)">Prithi Chand</a> is remembered in the Sikh tradition as vehemently opposing Guru Arjan, creating a faction Sikh community which the Sikhs following Guru Arjan called as <i>Minas</i> (literally, "scoundrels").<sup id="cite_ref-fenech39_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fenech39-207">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McLeod2009p20_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2009p20-208">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Guru Arjan is remembered in the Sikh for many things. He built the first <a href="/wiki/Harimandir_Sahib" class="mw-redirect" title="Harimandir Sahib">Harimandir Sahib</a> (later to become the Golden Temple). He was a poet and created the first edition of Sikh sacred text known as the <a href="/wiki/Adi_Granth" class="mw-redirect" title="Adi Granth">Ādi Granth</a> (literally <i>the first book</i>) and included the writings of the first five gurus and other enlightened 13 Hindu and 2 Muslim Sufi saints. In 1606, he was tortured and killed by the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal</a> emperor <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> for refusing to convert to Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pashauraarjan_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pashauraarjan-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> His martyrdom is considered a watershed event in the history of Sikhism.<sup id="cite_ref-pashauraarjan_28-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pashauraarjan-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_advancement">Political advancement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Political advancement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the martyrdom of Guru Arjan, his son <a href="/wiki/Guru_Hargobind" title="Guru Hargobind">Guru Hargobind</a> at age eleven became the sixth guru of the Sikhs and Sikhism dramatically evolved to become a political movement in addition to being religious.<sup id="cite_ref-pashaura29_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pashaura29-213">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> Guru Hargobind carried two swords, calling one spiritual and the other for temporal purpose (known as <i>mīrī</i> and <i>pīrī</i> in Sikhism).<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (December 2017)">self-published source</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> According to the Sikh tradition, Guru Arjan asked his son Hargobind to start a military tradition to protect the Sikh people and always keep himself surrounded by armed Sikhs. The building of an armed Sikh militia began with Guru Hargobind.<sup id="cite_ref-pashaura29_213-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pashaura29-213">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> Guru Hargobind was soon arrested by the Mughals and kept in jail in Gwalior. It is unclear how many years he served in prison, with different texts stating it to be between 2 and 12 years.<sup id="cite_ref-mandair48_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mandair48-215">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> He married three women, built a fort to defend Ramdaspur and created a formal court called <a href="/wiki/Akal_Takht" title="Akal Takht">Akal Takht</a>, now the highest Khalsa Sikh religious authority.<sup id="cite_ref-Phyllis2004_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phyllis2004-216">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1644, Guru Hargobind named his grandson <a href="/wiki/Guru_Har_Rai" title="Guru Har Rai">Har Rai</a> as the guru. The Mughal Emperor <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a> attempted political means to undermine the Sikh tradition, by dividing and influencing the succession.<sup id="cite_ref-mandair49_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mandair49-217">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> The Mughal ruler gave land grants to Dhir Mal, a grandson of Guru Hargobind living in Kartarpur, and attempted to encourage Sikhs to recognise Dhir Mal as the rightful successor to Guru Hargobind.<sup id="cite_ref-mandair49_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mandair49-217">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> Dhir Mal issued statements in favour of the Mughal state, and critical of his grandfather <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a>. Guru Hargobind rejected Dhir Mal, the later refused to give up the original version of the Adi Granth he had, and the Sikh community was divided.<sup id="cite_ref-mandair49_217-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mandair49-217">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Guru Har Rai is famed to have met Dara Shikoh during a time Dara Shikoh and his younger brother Aurangzeb were in a bitter succession fight. Aurangzeb summoned Guru Har Rai, who refused to go and sent his elder son Ram Rai instead.<sup id="cite_ref-McLeod2014p260_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2014p260-218">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> The emperor found a verse in the Sikh scripture insulting to Muslims, and Ram Rai agreed it was a mistake then changed it. Ram Rai thus pleased Aurangzeb, but displeased Guru Har Rai who excommunicated his elder son. He nominated his younger son <a href="/wiki/Guru_Har_Krishan" title="Guru Har Krishan">Guru Har Krishan</a> to succeed him in 1661. Aurangzeb responded by granting Ram Rai a <a href="/wiki/Jagir" title="Jagir">jagir</a> (land grant). Ram Rai founded a town there and enjoyed Aurangzeb's patronage, the town came to be known as Dehradun, after <i>Dehra</i> referring to Ram Rai's shrine. Sikhs who followed Ram Rai came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Ramraiya" title="Ramraiya">Ramraiya</a> Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-McLeod2014p260_218-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2014p260-218">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Guru Har Krishan became the eighth Guru at the age of five, and died of smallpox before reaching the age of eight. No hymns composed by these three gurus are included in the Guru Granth Sahib.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Guru_Tegh_Bahadur" title="Guru Tegh Bahadur">Guru Tegh Bahadur</a>, the uncle of Guru Har Krishan, became Guru in 1665. Tegh Bahadur resisted the forced conversions of <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmiri</a> <a href="/wiki/Pandit" title="Pandit">Pandits</a><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> and non-Muslims<sup id="cite_ref-Mandair2013p53_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair2013p53-223">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, and was publicly beheaded in 1675 on the orders of <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a> in <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a> for refusing to convert to Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-cs2013_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cs2013-224">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fenech4_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fenech4-225">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> His beheading traumatized the Sikhs. His body was cremated in Delhi, the head was carried secretively by Sikhs and cremated in <a href="/wiki/Anandpur_Sahib" title="Anandpur Sahib">Anandpur</a>. He was succeeded by his son, Gobind Rai who militarised his followers by creating the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a> in 1699, and baptising the <i><a href="/wiki/Panj_Piare" class="mw-redirect" title="Panj Piare">Pañj Piārē</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-MandairShackle2013p25_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MandairShackle2013p25-226">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> From then on, he was known as <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a>, and Sikh identity was redefined into a political force resisting religious persecution.<sup id="cite_ref-ws1981_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ws1981-227">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikh_confederacy_and_the_rise_of_the_Khalsa">Sikh confederacy and the rise of the Khalsa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Sikh confederacy and the rise of the Khalsa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a> inaugurated the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a> (the collective body of all <a href="/wiki/Amrit_Sanskar" class="mw-redirect" title="Amrit Sanskar">initiated Sikhs</a>) as the Sikh temporal authority in the year 1699. It created a community that combines its spiritual purpose and goals with political and military duties.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-granthfinalguru_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-granthfinalguru-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-parrinderp259_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parrinderp259-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Shortly before his death, Guru Gobind Singh proclaimed the <a href="/wiki/Guru_Granth_Sahib" title="Guru Granth Sahib">Gurū Granth Sāhib</a> (the Sikh Holy Scripture) to be the ultimate spiritual authority for the Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bodyguard_of_Ranjit_Singh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bodyguard_of_Ranjit_Singh.jpg/200px-Bodyguard_of_Ranjit_Singh.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="305" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bodyguard_of_Ranjit_Singh.jpg/300px-Bodyguard_of_Ranjit_Singh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bodyguard_of_Ranjit_Singh.jpg/400px-Bodyguard_of_Ranjit_Singh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="503" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bodyguard_of_Ranjit_Singh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Some bodyguards of <a href="/wiki/Ranjit_Singh" title="Ranjit Singh">Maharaja Ranjit Singh</a> at the Sikh capital, Lahore, Punjab</div></div></div>
<p>The Sikh Khalsa's rise to power began in the 17th century during a time of growing militancy against Mughal rule. The creation of a <a href="/wiki/Sikh_Empire" title="Sikh Empire">Sikh Empire</a> began when Guru Gobind Singh sent a Sikh general, <a href="/wiki/Banda_Singh_Bahadur" title="Banda Singh Bahadur">Banda Singh Bahadur</a>, to fight the Mughal rulers of India<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (December 2017)">self-published source</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and those who had committed atrocities against <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pir_Buddhu_Shah&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Pir Buddhu Shah (page does not exist)">Pir Buddhu Shah</a>. Banda Singh advanced his army towards the main Muslim Mughal city of Sirhind and, following the instructions of the guru, punished all the culprits. Soon after the invasion of Sirhind, while resting in his chamber after the Rehras prayer Guru Gobind Singh was stabbed by a <a href="/wiki/Pathan" class="mw-redirect" title="Pathan">Pathan</a> assassin hired by <a href="/wiki/Mughals" class="mw-redirect" title="Mughals">Mughals</a>. Gobind Singh killed the attacker with his sword. Though a European surgeon stitched the Guru's wound, the wound re-opened as the Guru tugged at a hard strong bow after a few days, causing profuse bleeding that led to Gobind Singh's death.
</p><p>After the Guru's death, Baba <a href="/wiki/Banda_Singh_Bahadur" title="Banda Singh Bahadur">Banda Singh Bahadur</a> became the commander-in-chief of the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> He organised the civilian rebellion and abolished or halted the <a href="/wiki/Zamindar" title="Zamindar">Zamindari</a> system in time he was active and gave the farmers <a href="/wiki/Self-ownership" title="Self-ownership">proprietorship</a> of their own land.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> Banda Singh was executed by the emperor <a href="/wiki/Farrukhsiyar" title="Farrukhsiyar">Farrukh Siyar</a> after refusing the offer of a pardon if he converted to Islam. The <a href="/wiki/Sikh_Confederacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikh Confederacy">confederacy of Sikh warrior bands</a> known as <i><a href="/wiki/Misls" class="mw-redirect" title="Misls">misls</a></i> emerged, but these fought between themselves. Ranjit Singh achieved a series of military victories and created a <a href="/wiki/Sikh_Empire" title="Sikh Empire">Sikh Empire</a> in 1799.
</p><p>The Sikh empire had its capital in <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, spread over almost 200,000 square miles (520,000 square kilometres) comprising what is now northwestern <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. The Sikh Empire entered into a treaty with the colonial British powers, with each side recognizing Sutlej River as the line of control and agreeing not to invade the other side.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> Ranjit Singh's most lasting legacy was the restoration and expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Harmandir_Sahib" class="mw-redirect" title="Harmandir Sahib">Harmandir Sahib</a>, most revered <a href="/wiki/Gurudwara" class="mw-redirect" title="Gurudwara">Gurudwara</a> of the Sikhs, with marble and gold, from which the popular name of the "Golden Temple" is derived.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> After the death of Ranjit Singh in 1839, the Sikh Empire fell into disorder. Ranjit Singh had failed to establish a lasting structure for Sikh government or stable succession, and the Sikh Empire rapidly declined after his death. Factions divided the Sikhs, and led to <a href="/wiki/First_Anglo-Sikh_War" title="First Anglo-Sikh War">Anglo-Sikh wars</a>. The British easily defeated the confused and demoralised <a href="/wiki/Sikh_Khalsa_Army" title="Sikh Khalsa Army">Khalsa forces</a>, then disbanded them into destitution.<sup id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994p207_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994p207-235">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> The youngest son of Ranjit Singh named <a href="/wiki/Maharaja_Duleep_Singh" class="mw-redirect" title="Maharaja Duleep Singh">Duleep Singh</a> ultimately succeeded, but he was arrested and exiled after the defeat of Sikh Khalsa.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Singh_Sabha_movement">Singh Sabha movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Singh Sabha movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Singh_Sabha_Movement" title="Singh Sabha Movement">Singh Sabha Movement</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Singh_Sabha_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Singh Sabha movement">Singh Sabha movement</a>, a movement to revitalize Sikhism, also saw the resurgence of the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">Khalsa</a> after their defeat by the British in the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Sikh_wars" title="Anglo-Sikh wars">Anglo-Sikh wars</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup>  and the subsequent decline and corruption of Sikh institutions during colonial rule, and the proselytization of other faith groups in the Punjab.<sup id="cite_ref-barrierssm_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barrierssm-238">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online-239">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup>  It was started in the 1870s, and after a period of interfactional rivalry, united under the Tat Khalsa to reinvigorate Sikh practice and institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-perplexed84_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perplexed84-240">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The last Maharaja of the Sikh Empire Duleep Singh converted to Christianity in 1853, a controversial but influential event in Sikh history. Along with his conversion, and after Sikh Empire had been dissolved and the region made a part of the colonial British Empire, <a href="/wiki/Proselytism" title="Proselytism">proselytising</a> activities of <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brahmo_Samaj" title="Brahmo Samaj">Brahmo Samajis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arya_Samaj" title="Arya Samaj">Arya Samaj</a>, Muslim Anjuman-i-Islamia and Ahmadiyah sought to convert the Sikhs in northwestern Indian subcontinent into their respective faiths.<sup id="cite_ref-barrierssm_238-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barrierssm-238">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online_239-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online-239">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> These developments launched the <a href="/wiki/Singh_Sabha_Movement" title="Singh Sabha Movement">Singh Sabha Movement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-barrierssm_238-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barrierssm-238">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online_239-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online-239">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first meeting of the movement was in the Golden Temple, Amritsar in 1873, and it was largely launched by the <a href="/wiki/Sanatan_Sikh" title="Sanatan Sikh">Sanatan Sikhs</a>, Gianis, priests, and granthis.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Shortly thereafter, Nihang Sikhs began influencing the movement, followed by a sustained campaign by the <a href="/wiki/Tat_Khalsa" title="Tat Khalsa">Tat Khalsa</a>, which had quickly gained dominance by the early 1880s.<sup id="cite_ref-perplexed84_240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perplexed84-240">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994-242">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> The movement became a struggle between Sanatan Sikhs and Tat Khalsa in defining and interpreting Sikhism.<sup id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p28_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p28-243">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fenech273_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fenech273-245">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sanatan Sikhs led by <a href="/wiki/Khem_Singh_Bedi" title="Khem Singh Bedi">Khem Singh Bedi</a> – who claimed to be a direct descendant of Guru Nanak, Avtar Singh Vahiria and others supported a more inclusive approach which considered Sikhism as a reformed tradition of Hinduism, while Tat Khalsa campaigned for an exclusive approach to the Sikh identity, disagreeing with Sanatan Sikhs and seeking to modernize Sikhism.<sup id="cite_ref-fenech273_245-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fenech273-245">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994_242-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994-242">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mandair2013p82_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandair2013p82-246">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> The Sikh Sabha movement expanded in north and northwest Indian subcontinent, leading to more than 100 Singh Sabhas.<sup id="cite_ref-fenech273_245-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fenech273-245">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p28_243-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p28-243">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> By the early decades of the 20th century, the influence of Tat Khalsa increased in interpreting the nature of Sikhism and their control over the Sikh Gurdwaras.<sup id="cite_ref-fenech273_245-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fenech273-245">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p28_243-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p28-243">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oberoi1994_242-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oberoi1994-242">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> The Tat Khalsa banished Brahmanical practices including the use of the <i><a href="/wiki/Yajna" title="Yajna">yagna</a></i> fire,<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> replaced by the <i><a href="/wiki/Anand_Karaj" title="Anand Karaj">Anand Karaj</a></i> marriage ceremony in accordance with Sikh scripture, and the idols and the images of Sikh gurus from the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Temple" title="Golden Temple">Golden Temple</a> in 1905, traditions which had taken root during the administration of the <i><a href="/wiki/Singh_Sabha_Movement#Colonial_rule" title="Singh Sabha Movement">mahants</a></i> during the 1800s.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> They undertook a sustained campaign to standardize how Sikh Gurdwaras looked and ran, while looking to Sikh scriptures and the early Sikh tradition<sup id="cite_ref-perplexed85_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perplexed85-250">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> to purify the Sikh identity.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The spiritual successors of the Singh Sabha include the <a href="/wiki/Akali_movement" title="Akali movement">Akali movement</a> of the 1920s, as well as the modern-day Shiromani Gurdwara Parbandhak Committee (<a href="/wiki/SGPC" class="mw-redirect" title="SGPC">SGPC</a>), a <a href="/wiki/Gurdwara" title="Gurdwara">gurdwara</a> administration body, and the <a href="/wiki/Akali_Dal" class="mw-redirect" title="Akali Dal">Akali Dal</a> political party.<sup id="cite_ref-SinghFenech2014p30_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SinghFenech2014p30-252">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Partition">Partition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Partition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Sikhs participated and contributed to the decades-long Indian independence movement from the colonial rule in the first half of the 20th century. Ultimately when the British Empire recognized independent India, the land was partitioned into Hindu majority India and Muslim majority Pakistan (East and West) in 1947. This event, states Banga, was a watershed event in Sikh history.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100-253">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shani2007p86_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shani2007p86-254">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> The Sikhs had historically lived in northwestern region of Indian subcontinent on both sides of the partition line ("Radcliffe line"). According to Banga and other scholars, the Sikhs had strongly opposed the Muslim League demands and saw it as "perpetuation of Muslim domination" and anti-Sikh policies in what just a 100 years before was a part of the Sikh Empire. During the discussions with the colonial authorities, Tara Singh emerged as an important leader who campaigned to prevent the partition and for the recognition of Sikhs as the third community.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100_253-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100-253">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> In 1940, a few Sikhs such as the victims of <a href="/wiki/Komagata_Maru_incident" title="Komagata Maru incident">Komagata Maru in Canada</a> proposed the idea of Khalistan as a buffer state between Pakistan and India.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–103_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–103-255">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> These leaders, however, were largely ignored.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100_253-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100-253">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shani2007p86_254-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shani2007p86-254">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> Many other Sikh leaders supported the partition along religious and demographic lines.<sup id="cite_ref-Mann2014p81_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mann2014p81-256">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When partition was announced, the newly created line divided the Sikh population into two-halves. The Sikhs suffered organized violence and riots against them in West Pakistan, and Sikhs moved en masse to the Indian side leaving behind their property and the sacred places of Sikhism. This reprisals on Sikhs were not one sided, because as Sikhs entered the Indian side, the Muslims in East Punjab experienced reprisals and they moved to West Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100_253-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100-253">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mann2014p81_256-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mann2014p81-256">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> Before the partition, Sikhs constituted about 15% of the population in West Punjab that became a part of Pakistan, the majority being Muslims (55%). The Sikhs were the economic elite and wealthiest in West Punjab, with them having the largest representation in West Punjab's aristocracy, nearly 700 Gurdwaras and 400 educational institutions that served the interests of the Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–103_255-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–103-255">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> Prior to the partition, there were a series of disputes between the majority Muslims and minority Sikhs, such as on the matters of <a href="/wiki/Jhatka" title="Jhatka">jhatka</a> versus <a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">halal</a> meat, the disputed ownership of <a href="/wiki/Shaheed_Ganj_Mosque" title="Shaheed Ganj Mosque">Gurdwara Sahidganj</a> in Lahore which Muslims sought as a mosque and Sikhs as a Gurdwara, and the insistence of the provincial Muslim government in switching from Indian <a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi" title="Gurmukhi">Gurmukhi</a> script to Arabic-Persian <a href="/wiki/Nastaliq" class="mw-redirect" title="Nastaliq">Nastaliq</a> script in schools.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100_253-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–100-253">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> During and after the Simla Conference in June 1945, headed by Lord Wavell, the Sikh leaders initially expressed their desire to be recognized as the third party, but ultimately relegated their demands and sought a United India where Sikhs, Hindus and Muslims would live together, under a Swiss style constitution. The Muslim League rejected this approach, demanding that entire Punjab should be granted to Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–104_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–104-257">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> The Sikh leaders then sought the partition instead, and Congress Working Committee passed a resolution in support of partitioning Punjab and Bengal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–104_257-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga201799–104-257">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg/220px-Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg/330px-Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg/440px-Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3268" data-file-height="2187" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sikh_Light_Infantry.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sikh_Light_Infantry" title="Sikh Light Infantry">Sikh Light Infantry</a> personnel march past during the Republic day parade in <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi" title="New Delhi">New Delhi</a>, India</div></div></div>
<p>Between March and August 1947, a series of riots, arson, plunder of Sikh property, assassination of Sikh leaders, and killings in Jhelum districts, Rawalpindi, Attock and other places made Tara Singh call the situation in Punjab as "civil war", while Lord Mountbatten stated "civil war preparations were going on". The riots had triggered the early waves of migration in April, with some 20,000 people leaving northwest Punjab and moving to Patiala.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga2017104–105_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga2017104–105-258">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shani2007p86_254-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shani2007p86-254">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> In Rawalpindi, 40,000 people became homeless. The Sikh leaders made desperate petitions, but all religious communities were suffering in the political turmoil. Sikhs, states Banga, were "only 4 million out of a total of 28&#160;million in Punjab, and 6&#160;million out of nearly 400&#160;million in India; they did not constitute the majority, not even in a single district".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga2017104–105_258-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga2017104–105-258">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wolpert2010p9_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolpert2010p9-259">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When the partition line was formally announced in August 1947, the violence was unprecedented, with Sikhs being one of the most affected religious community both in terms of deaths, as well as property loss, injury, trauma and disruption.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga2017108–111_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga2017108–111-260">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mann2014p81_256-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mann2014p81-256">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> Sikhs and Muslims were both victims and perpetrators of retaliatory violence against each other. Estimates range between 200,000 and 2 million deaths of Sikhs, Hindus and Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga2017108–111_260-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga2017108–111-260">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mann2014p81_256-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mann2014p81-256">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> There were numerous rapes of and mass suicides by Sikh women, they being taken captives, their rescues and above all a mass exodus of Sikhs from newly created Pakistan into newly created India. The partition created the "largest foot convoy of refugees recorded in [human] history, stretching over 100 kilometer long", states Banga, with nearly 300,000 people consisting of mostly "distraught, suffering, injured and angry Sikhs". Sikh and Hindu refugees from Pakistan flooded into India, Muslim refugees from India flooded into Pakistan, each into their new homeland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanga2017108–111_260-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanga2017108–111-260">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wolpert2010p9_259-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolpert2010p9-259">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Khalistan">Khalistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Khalistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg/220px-Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg/330px-Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg/440px-Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg 2x" data-file-width="608" data-file-height="456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thousands-Sikhs-protest-in-London.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sikhs in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> protesting against the Indian government</div></div></div>
<p>The early 1980s witnessed some Sikh groups seeking an independent nation named <a href="/wiki/Khalistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Khalistan">Khalistan</a> carved out from India and Pakistan. The Golden Temple and Akal Takht were occupied by various militant groups in 1982. These included the <a href="/wiki/Dharam_Yudh_Morcha" class="mw-redirect" title="Dharam Yudh Morcha">Dharam Yudh Morcha</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Jarnail_Singh_Bhindranwale" title="Jarnail Singh Bhindranwale">Jarnail Singh Bhindranwale</a>, the Babbar Khalsa, the AISSF and the National Council of Khalistan.<sup id="cite_ref-Chima2008p85_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chima2008p85-261">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> Between 1982 and 1983, there were <a href="/wiki/Anandpur_Resolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Anandpur Resolution">Anandpur Resolution</a> demand-related terrorist attacks against civilians in parts of India.<sup id="cite_ref-horowitz482_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-horowitz482-262">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> By late 1983, the Bhindranwale led group had begun to build bunkers and observations posts in and around the Golden Temple, with militants involved in weapons training.<sup id="cite_ref-Chima2008p85_261-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chima2008p85-261">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> In June 1984, the then <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_India" title="Prime Minister of India">Prime Minister of India</a> <a href="/wiki/Indira_Gandhi" title="Indira Gandhi">Indira Gandhi</a> ordered Indian Army to begin <a href="/wiki/Operation_Blue_Star" title="Operation Blue Star">Operation Blue Star</a> against the militants.<sup id="cite_ref-Chima2008p85_261-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chima2008p85-261">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> The fierce engagement took place in the precincts of Darbar Sahib and resulted in many deaths, including Bhindranwale, the destruction of the Sikh Reference Library, which was considered a national treasure that contained over a thousand rare manuscripts,<sup id="cite_ref-mann_114_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mann_114-263">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> and destroyed Akal Takht. Numerous soldiers, civilians and militants died in the cross fire. Within days of the Operation Bluestar, some 2,000 Sikh soldiers in India mutinied and attempted to reach Amritsar to liberate the Golden Temple.<sup id="cite_ref-Chima2008p85_261-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chima2008p85-261">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> Within six months, on 31 October 1984, Indira Gandhi's Sikh bodyguards <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Indira_Gandhi" title="Assassination of Indira Gandhi">assassinated her</a>. The assassination triggered the <a href="/wiki/1984_anti-Sikh_riots" title="1984 anti-Sikh riots">1984 anti-Sikh riots</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-horowitz482_262-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-horowitz482-262">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> According to Donald Horowitz, while anti-Sikh riots led to much damage and deaths, many serious provocations by militants also failed to trigger ethnic violence in many cases throughout the 1980s. The Sikhs and their neighbors, for most part, ignored attempts to provoke riots and communal strife.<sup id="cite_ref-horowitz482_262-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-horowitz482-262">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikh_people">Sikh people</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sikhism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Sikh people">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
